    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3143199302" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3143199302" />
    <a title="Jesús  Erro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40291334-jes-s-erro"><img alt="Jesús  Erro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602895566p2/40291334.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3143199302?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jesús  Erro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jesús  Erro" href="/user/show/40291334-jes-s-erro">Jesús  Erro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/30646532-una-pena-en-observacion">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anybody going through the death, bereavement or separation of a loved one</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jesús  by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/70353645-irene-maci">Irene Maciá</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40291334-jes-s-erro?shelf=catholic">catholic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40291334-jes-s-erro?shelf=anguish">anguish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40291334-jes-s-erro?shelf=suffering">suffering</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40291334-jes-s-erro?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40291334-jes-s-erro?shelf=hope">hope</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40291334-jes-s-erro?shelf=loss">loss</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40291334-jes-s-erro?shelf=love">love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40291334-jes-s-erro?shelf=virtues">virtues</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40291334-jes-s-erro?shelf=marriage">marriage</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40291334-jes-s-erro?shelf=doubt">doubt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3143199302" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8760691524511043182">ENGLISH: The crudeness of <i>Psalm 22 - “Eli, Eli lama sabactani?”</i> - lived by a twentieth century's human being - is what you can enjoy in this wonderful book. This is a brief autobiographical essay about <i>C.S. Lewis's</i> grief over his wife's death. 52 heartbreaking pages where the author survives the bereavement while creating and modeling a hope based on <i>Christianity</i>. The teaching of the book is extrapolable to losses or emotional separations.<br/><br/>The book is presented as a <i>paradigm of Psalm 22</i>, as it be</span>
  <span id="freeText8760691524511043182" style="display:none">ENGLISH: The crudeness of <i>Psalm 22 - “Eli, Eli lama sabactani?”</i> - lived by a twentieth century's human being - is what you can enjoy in this wonderful book. This is a brief autobiographical essay about <i>C.S. Lewis's</i> grief over his wife's death. 52 heartbreaking pages where the author survives the bereavement while creating and modeling a hope based on <i>Christianity</i>. The teaching of the book is extrapolable to losses or emotional separations.<br /><br />The book is presented as a <i>paradigm of Psalm 22</i>, as it begins with a desperate cry due to the apparent abandonment of God. It also talks about the power of <i>praise</i> as a form of union with the transcendent. My first <i>Lewis</i> has impressed me: a clear essay, beautiful, deep and with no artifice, which doesn't remain in the metaphysical but falls down to the ordinary life. According to the author, the greatest <i>marriage gift</i> is loving people's reality: with its barriers, defects and unpredictability. That is, not to love our partner's memories or images but their direct and independent reality.<br /><br />The book is divided into four chapters. In <b>first</b> chapter, the author begins by describing the immediate external symptoms of grief: the overwhelming <i>laziness</i> that permeates all areas of life, the neglect of body grooming, the permanent memory of the deceased, the request for a certainty that confirms that she continues to live in the <i>Hereafter</i> or the question <i>Where is God in the midst of so much suffering? </i>.<br /><br />In <b>chapter two</b>, the author climbs a step on the <i>spiritual ladder of the grief</i>. Because of his wife's physical absence, only his own images on her remain. The question is: <i>Where is she?</i> The author defines <i>"earthly existence"</i> as: <i>"a Prelude to Something Bigger."</i> In the face of emotional grief, a healthy <i>relativization exercise</i> is always purifying. <i>Would it really be good for the deceased to return to earth?</i> It's obvious that she won't return - reality <i>"never plays the same tune twice"</i> - then we begin to question the goodness of God, attributing to Him our evil characteristics: <i>Is God really bad or is our perception?</i><br /><br />In <b>chapter three</b> the previous question is answered. Our right to grumling is legitimate but it doesn't solve anything. It's also selfish because we only seek our <i>own past's restoration</i>, not the wellness of the dead. The generosity of our suffering is challenged. Only Jesus Christ died for us: <i>Would we be able to do the same for the deceased?</i> We conclude that our earthly grief is not pure, it doesn't help the dead: we must accept reality, abandon ourselves to the will of God and let her go.<br /><br />In <b>chapter four</b> we recognize the benefits of suffering, basically inner growth: the continuous reconstruction of the image of the dead provides greater clarity of her life in earth. That is, we have the advantage of an <i>Iconoclastic Reality</i> that facilitates a better approach to the deceased and to God through <i>praise</i>. At this point, <i>Lewis</i> gives us an unique experience of <i>extrasensory communication</i> with his dead wife. An experience - devoid of all emotions - based on his wife's intellect and will, makes <i>Lewis</i> realize the incompleteness of our knowledge of the universe. The author is tempted to postulate a society based on <i>pure intelligence</i>. I suspect that <i>Lewis</i> refers to the knowledge implied by <i>True Love</i>: loving involves knowledge and it is done through intelligence.<br /><br />Our intellect's poverty leads the author to reconsider and accept the validity of <i>Christian doctrine</i>. The silence and apparent abandonment of God have justification, God ceases to be that <i>"Cosmic Sadist"</i>. <i>Lewis</i> confirms this thought by remembering his wife's reconciliation with God just before she dies on his deathbed. This leads him to conclude the book with a hopeful statement: <i>"How cruel it would be to summon the dead if we could do it!"</i><br /><br />More information at <a href="https://jesuserro.com/2020/01/26/una-pena-en-observacion-por-cs-lewis/" rel="nofollow noopener"> this article </a> of my blog.<br /><br /><br />ESPAÑOL: La crudeza del <i>Salmo 22 - “Elí, Elí ¿lama sabactani?”</i> – puesta en boca de un hombre del siglo XX – es lo que puedes saborear en este maravilloso libro. Se trata de un breve ensayo autobiográfico sobre el duelo por el fallecimiento de la esposa del autor. 52 páginas desgarradoras en las que <i>C.S. Lewis</i> sobrevive el duelo a la vez que crea y modela una esperanza basada en el <i>Cristianismo</i>. La enseñanza del libro es extrapolable a las pérdidas o separaciones afectivas.<br /><br />El libro se presenta como <i>paradigma del Salmo 22</i>, en el sentido de que comienza con un grito desesperado ante el aparente abandono de Dios. Habla también del poder de la <i>alabanza</i> como forma de unión con lo trascendente. Mi primer <i>Lewis</i> me ha impresionado: es un ensayo claro, bello, profundo y sin artificios, que no se queda en lo metafísico sino que se abaja a lo terrenal de lo afectivo. Según el autor, el mayor regalo del matrimonio es amar la realidad de las personas: con sus barreras, defectos e imprevisibilidad. Es decir, no amar imágenes o recuerdos de nuestra pareja sino su directa e independiente realidad.<br /><br />El libro se divide en cuatro capítulos. En el <b>primero</b>, el autor comienza describiendo lo inmediato, los síntomas externos del duelo: esa <i>desidia</i> abrumadora que permea todos los ámbitos de la vida, el descuido del aseo corporal, el recuerdo permanente de ella en todo lo que hacemos, la petición de una certeza que confirme que sigue viviendo en el <i>Más Allá</i> o la pregunta de dónde está Dios en medio de tanto sufrimiento.<br /><br />En el <b>capítulo dos</b>, el autor sube un peldaño en la <i>escalera espiritual del duelo</i>. Ante la ausencia física del fallecido, sólo permanecen las imágenes basadas en su recuerdo. La pregunta es: <i>¿dónde está?</i> El autor define la existencia terrena como un: <i>“preludio de algo más grande”</i>. Ante el duelo afectivo, un sano <i>ejercicio de relativización</i> es siempre purificador. <i>¿Realmente sería bueno para el fallecido regresar a la realidad terrestre?</i> Es evidente que no volverá – la realidad <i>“nunca toca dos veces la misma melodía”</i> – entonces comenzamos a cuestionar la bondad de Dios atribuyéndole nuestras características de la maldad: <i>¿realmente Dios es malo o es nuestra percepción?</i><br /><br />En el <b>capítulo tres</b> se responde a la pregunta anterior. Nuestro derecho al pataleo es legítimo pero no resuelve nada. Además es egoísta porque sólo buscamos la <i>restauración de nuestro propio pasado</i>, no el bien del difunto. Se pone en duda la generosidad de nuestro sufrimiento. Sólo <i>Jesucristo</i> llegó a morir por nosotros: <i>¿seríamos capaces de hacer lo mismo por el fallecido?</i> Llegamos a la conclusión de que nuestro dolor terrenal no es puro, no ayuda al difunto: tenemos que aceptar la realidad, abandonarnos a la voluntad de Dios y dejarla ir.<br /><br />En el <b>capítulo cuatro</b> reconocemos los beneficios del sufrimiento, básicamente el <i>crecimiento interior</i>: la continua reconstrucción de la imagen del difunto proporciona una mayor claridad de lo que fue su vida. Es decir, tenemos la ventaja de una <i>realidad iconoclasta</i> que facilita un mejor acercamiento al fallecido y a Dios a través de la <i>alabanza</i>. En este punto, <i>Lewis</i> nos regala una singular experiencia de <i>comunicación extrasensorial</i> con su esposa fallecida. Una experiencia carente de toda emotividad basada en el intelecto y la voluntad de la fallecida, le hace ver a <i>Lewis</i> lo incompleto de nuestro conocimiento del universo. El autor es tentado a postular una sociedad basada en la inteligencia. Sospecho que <i>Lewis</i> se refiere al conocimiento que implica el <i>amor verdadero</i> – para amar hay que conocer y se hace a través de la inteligencia.<br /><br />La humildad ante la pobreza de nuestro intelecto, lleva al autor a reconsiderar y aceptar la validez de la <i>doctrina cristiana</i>. El silencio y aparente abandono de Dios tienen justificación, Dios deja de ser ese <i>“Sádico Cósmico”</i>. <i>Lewis</i> confirma este pensamiento durante la reconciliación de su amada con Dios justo antes de que ésta fallezca en su lecho de muerte. Lo que le lleva a concluir el libro con un esperanzador: <i>“¡Qué cruel sería convocar a los muertos caso de que pudiéramos hacerlo!”</i><br /><br />Más información en <a href="https://jesuserro.com/2020/01/26/una-pena-en-observacion-por-cs-lewis/" rel="nofollow noopener">este artículo</a> de mi blog.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8760691524511043182" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3329198045" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3329198045" />
    <a title="Timothy Urges" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13015682-timothy-urges"><img alt="Timothy Urges" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563309359p2/13015682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3329198045?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Timothy Urges" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Timothy Urges" href="/user/show/13015682-timothy-urges">Timothy Urges</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060652" href="/book/show/9405925-a-grief-observed">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3329198045" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15643988654940534658"><i>No one ever told me that grief felt so like fear. I am not afraid, but the sensation is like being afraid. The same fluttering in the stomach, the same restlessness, the yawning. I keep on swallowing. <br/>At other times it feels like being mildly drunk, or concussed. There is a sort of invisible blanket between the world and me. I find it hard to take in what anyone says. Or perhaps, hard to want to take it in. It is so uninteresting. Yet I want the others to be about me. I dread the moments when th</i></span>
  <span id="freeText15643988654940534658" style="display:none"><i>No one ever told me that grief felt so like fear. I am not afraid, but the sensation is like being afraid. The same fluttering in the stomach, the same restlessness, the yawning. I keep on swallowing. <br />At other times it feels like being mildly drunk, or concussed. There is a sort of invisible blanket between the world and me. I find it hard to take in what anyone says. Or perhaps, hard to want to take it in. It is so uninteresting. Yet I want the others to be about me. I dread the moments when the house is empty. If only they would talk to one another and not to me. </i><br /><br />In my nearly 30 years of existence I have yet to experience any great loss. But I know one day it will come and one day I will cope. <br /><br />I have, however, experienced depression, and Lewis’ explanation of grief succinctly covers certain aspects of the illness: the apathy and the ennui that eats you alive. You don’t care, and yet you care too much. It becomes a pit of tar that’s inescapable, but the thought of escaping is terrifying because that would result in change. And change is hard to face when you can’t even bring yourself to brush your teeth. Depression is much more than sadness, but sadness plays its part. <br /><br />I’m much better than I was in my younger years, but Lewis brought back some feelings that were once hard to acknowledge. You don’t realize how bad it is until you’re out of it. <br /><br /><i>To make an organism which is also a spirit; to make that terrible oxymoron, a ‘spiritual animal.’ To take a poor primate, a beast with nerve-endings all over it, a creature with a stomach that wants to be filled, a breeding animal that wants its mate, and say, ‘Now get on with it. Become a god.’</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="15643988654940534658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_174734877" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/174734877" />
    <a title="Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder"><img alt="Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572373103p2/3048260.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/174734877?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*" href="/user/show/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder">Erin *Proud Book Hoarder*</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060652388" href="/book/show/49221.A_Grief_Observed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=a-own-book-form">a-own-book-form</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=nonfiction-i-stayed-awake-through">nonfiction-i-stayed-awake-through</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=got-in-2014">got-in-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=christianity">christianity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3048260-erin-proud-book-hoarder?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer174734877" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17638972513749949673">It's hard to rate a book like this - doesn't feel fully appropriate since it's more an internal dialogue through stages of grief than anything else. C.S. Lewis was always a talented writer, whether penning fiction or non, but this is a diary-style jotting of internal reflections during the horrible stages of losing his wife to cancer. <br/><br/>Written in mini paragraphs that were apparently sections recorded during his thoughts, I can almost picture him waking up at night and unable to go back to sleep, </span>
  <span id="freeText17638972513749949673" style="display:none">It's hard to rate a book like this - doesn't feel fully appropriate since it's more an internal dialogue through stages of grief than anything else. C.S. Lewis was always a talented writer, whether penning fiction or non, but this is a diary-style jotting of internal reflections during the horrible stages of losing his wife to cancer. <br /><br />Written in mini paragraphs that were apparently sections recorded during his thoughts, I can almost picture him waking up at night and unable to go back to sleep, reflecting on something in particular, then casually writing it down on a notebook he kept on the bedside table.<br /><br />I've read reviews where he goes through phases of grief, anger, and then an almost acceptance. I didn't feel the acceptance as much per se, but more of a fondness for her memory and realizing that to rely too much on memory is never enough.<br /><br />It's a sad, sobering book that is helpful to read through a person's own grieving process. The raw feelings come clearly through the pages, scattered thoughts almost always disjointed by inner reflections as his mind tries to heal through the trauma he faced. <br /><br />The book is of course not 'enjoyable' - it's not a self-help, advice book on grief either. It's a personal process that has been shared, a painful experience that clearly comes through his writing. Recommended for those who are going through grief, sometimes sharing through reading and writing is the best form of therapy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17638972513749949673" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_121963268" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/121963268" />
    <a title="Louize" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3019973-louize"><img alt="Louize" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348845553p2/3019973.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/121963268?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Louize" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Louize" href="/user/show/3019973-louize">Louize</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780786178612" href="/book/show/184242.A_Grief_Observed">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer121963268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15182886661606775537">Unlike C.S. Lewis, it was my dad whom I lost 17 years ago; but when he said that <i>“grief felt so like fear”</i> in the beginning of his book, I believe I know what he meant ; or, to make it more precise, I think he knows exactly what he’s talking about.<br/><br/><b>A Grief Observed</b> offers a look at a man in deep despair, who doubted God because of it, but eventually emerges with a deep understanding of himself, his love for his departed wife, and of God.<br/><br/>In the first part, the question he presented is not <i>“Do God </i></span>
  <span id="freeText15182886661606775537" style="display:none">Unlike C.S. Lewis, it was my dad whom I lost 17 years ago; but when he said that <i>“grief felt so like fear”</i> in the beginning of his book, I believe I know what he meant ; or, to make it more precise, I think he knows exactly what he’s talking about.<br /><br /><b>A Grief Observed</b> offers a look at a man in deep despair, who doubted God because of it, but eventually emerges with a deep understanding of himself, his love for his departed wife, and of God.<br /><br />In the first part, the question he presented is not <i>“Do God really exist?”</i>, but rather <i>”Is God truly good?”</i> If so, then why is a good God would allow man to suffer?<br /><br />Along the way, he referred to God as the <i>Cosmic Sadist</i>. He said that God needs to shake and torture us for us to realize that we have some higher purpose. Although I wouldn’t be so bold as to call God a sadist, I do believe that He allows grief for us to experience and appreciate life more fully. Knowing both sides of the coin, grief and joy, allows us to see God’s face and acknowledge His presence in our lives. For indeed on our own, faith is like a house of cards build on thin air, until God knocks it down and call us to build on a firm foundation.<br /><br />During his slow recuperation from sorrow, Lewis tackles the question <i>”How can one overcome grief without betraying the departed?”</i><br /><br /><i>”There is no denying that in some sense I feel better; and with that comes at once a sort of shame…”</i> In this I have to fully agree with his reasoning. It is true that prolonging our unhappiness does not convey our deepest love for the departed, nor do we give justice to their memories. That in sorrow, we can have no distinctive memory of them; that every image we conjure of them is canceled by our miseries, leaving us more confused. It is when we are at peace that we remember them more clearly, sometimes leaving a smile to our lips.<br /><br />In his reconciliation with God, Lewis mentioned the perfection of things, like marriage. Therefore, such things could not be prolonged and we then have to move on. This fact really hit me most. That no matter how close and perfect my husband and I are for each other, death will separate us. <i>“It was too perfect to last”</i>, he said. The mere thought of it sends shiver through me. My only recourse is to hold on to God’s promise of hope; Christ will gather us all in his return. We will be together again.<br /><br />Lastly, he mentioned, <i>”Now get on with it. Become a god.”</i> Apostle Paul said as much in his epistle to the missionary churches. <br /><br />Most people think that we are but a handful of dust to begin with and to dust we shall return in the end. For anyone else who believes that we are created in the image of God, our goal should be to know more &amp; more about Christ, and strive to emulate him at all times. We do have a higher purpose, that life has a meaning and so is death.<br /><br />The first part of this book is set in Lewis’ very deep anger at God which shows, and so requires an amount of discernment; and the final part is a good source of encouragement. A Grief Observed is a great book for those in deep sorrow because of loss, and for those who are questioning their faith.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15182886661606775537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Perkins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Perkins" href="/user/show/13298726-michael-perkins">Michael Perkins</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1105386638" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16175787393657003575">I read most of the Lewis "canon" when I was in my 20's. I am now the same age Lewis was when he died. Consequently, I have lived a lot of life, mixed in with a whole range of people, read a lot, traveled a lot. Got married. Raised a family. Lost loved ones. I know and have experienced a good deal more than when I was in my 20's.<br/><br/>Having said that, I think this book is his most honest and genuine book. I have attempted to pick up some old Lewis, The Four Loves, Surprised by Joy, and see that the to</span>
  <span id="freeText16175787393657003575" style="display:none">I read most of the Lewis "canon" when I was in my 20's. I am now the same age Lewis was when he died. Consequently, I have lived a lot of life, mixed in with a whole range of people, read a lot, traveled a lot. Got married. Raised a family. Lost loved ones. I know and have experienced a good deal more than when I was in my 20's.<br /><br />Having said that, I think this book is his most honest and genuine book. I have attempted to pick up some old Lewis, The Four Loves, Surprised by Joy, and see that the tone is very academic and rather lacking in feeling. It's the logic of a bachelor ivory tower Don who hung out with people exactly like himself.<br /><br />It was the entrance of Joy Davidman and her two sons into his life that put him in touch with humanity. And it was the loss of Joy that shattered all the stuffy nonsense of his arguments in the Problem of Pain and made him feel real pain to the point where he is angry with God and even refers to him as a Cosmic Sadist. This is the real world.<br /><br />Meanwhile, many fans of Lewis don't realize that he gave up on his Mere Christianity apologetics late in life. Surprised By Joy and his other apologetics were aimed at his peers, including The Inklings. When he found he could not change any of their minds, and even alienated Tolkien in the process, he discarded these arguments. He turned to writing The Chronicles of Narnia instead.<br /><br />-----------<br /><br />Interesting article on Lewis from The New Yorker....<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2005/11/21/prisoner-of-narnia">https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/20...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16175787393657003575" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2292617685" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2292617685" />
    <a title="Melora" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5586131-melora"><img alt="Melora" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548110965p2/5586131.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2292617685?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melora" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melora" href="/user/show/5586131-melora">Melora</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060652388" href="/book/show/49221.A_Grief_Observed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5586131-melora?shelf=death">death</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5586131-melora?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2292617685" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11602582629827534851">As Madeleine L'Engle says in her introduction, “each experience of grief is unique,” and Lewis was a quirky sort of fellow. His grieving for his wife, so dearly cherished during their far-too-brief marriage, is explored through the format of passionate journal entries. As with others of his works, I find that our thoughts on the issue of theodicy – the problem of pain and a benevolent, all-powerful God – aren't quite the same. Still, his experience of the progression of loss and pain, of struggl</span>
  <span id="freeText11602582629827534851" style="display:none">As Madeleine L'Engle says in her introduction, “each experience of grief is unique,” and Lewis was a quirky sort of fellow. His grieving for his wife, so dearly cherished during their far-too-brief marriage, is explored through the format of passionate journal entries. As with others of his works, I find that our thoughts on the issue of theodicy – the problem of pain and a benevolent, all-powerful God – aren't quite the same. Still, his experience of the progression of loss and pain, of struggle to reconcile belief and emotion, of fear of the loss of memories, etc., have elements which much surely be nearly universal, and his honesty is comforting.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11602582629827534851" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_118150111" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/118150111" />
    <a title="Tina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1552211-tina"><img alt="Tina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1333881094p2/1552211.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/118150111?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tina" href="/user/show/1552211-tina">Tina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6573893-a-grief-observed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1552211-tina?shelf=christian">christian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1552211-tina?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1552211-tina?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1552211-tina?shelf=2010">2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer118150111" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1314711170445718259"><strong>Original post at <a href='http://onemorepage.tinamats.com/a-grief-observed/' rel='nofollow noopener'>One More Page</a></strong><br/><br/>Just yesterday, I was chatting with one of my best friends who is also my old household head in Youth for Christ (YFC). She was telling me about her latest Kindle purchase (if you're curious, it's Cassandra Clare's <em>Clockwork Angel</em>). I told her about how I was reading <em>A Grief Observed </em>in my Kindle, and added that I wanted to buy other C.S. Lewis books there, too, because I realized that his books are a bit too expensive if I buy it here in full price, and I don't rea</span>
  <span id="freeText1314711170445718259" style="display:none"><strong>Original post at <a href="http://onemorepage.tinamats.com/a-grief-observed/" rel="nofollow noopener">One More Page</a></strong><br /><br />Just yesterday, I was chatting with one of my best friends who is also my old household head in Youth for Christ (YFC). She was telling me about her latest Kindle purchase (if you're curious, it's Cassandra Clare's <em>Clockwork Angel</em>). I told her about how I was reading <em>A Grief Observed </em>in my Kindle, and added that I wanted to buy other C.S. Lewis books there, too, because I realized that his books are a bit too expensive if I buy it here in full price, and I don't really have the patience to dig for them in bargain bookstores. My friend laughed (as much as you can online, anyway) and she said she's not ready for C.S. Lewis, at least not yet now. This is coming from my friend who would spend her spare time watching Hillsong United worship videos, mind you.<br /><br />Today I realized that I've read so many Christian books but I've never really read any of C.S. Lewis. It's not that I don't have his books, too. I have <strong>
  <em>Mere Christianity</em>
</strong> and the boxed set of <strong>
  <em>The Chronicles of Narnia</em>
</strong> but I haven't finished any of them. Strange? In a way, yes. But thinking about that and my conversation with my friend yesterday, I think it may not be <em>that</em> strange, because I realize that I may have not been ready to read C.S. Lewis' books back when I first got them.<br /><br />Truth be told, I wouldn't have gotten <em>A Grief Observed</em> if it wasn't one of the books for discussion in our Goodreads group. It's been a long time since I actually cracked open a non-fiction book, and whenever I do, I never finish them. Another reason why I would not have gotten this by myself is because I can't relate to grief, at least, not yet.<br /><br /><strong>I am a stranger to grief. </strong>Sure, I know some people who have passed away and I have shed tears for them, but I have never really felt the same kind of grief that I know other people have felt. The last closest relative I know who passed away was my maternal grandmother, and that's ten years ago, and all the other deaths I've heard about is not close enough to me for me to actually grieve the way other people do.<br /><br />But I'm not taking this one lightly. I still feel afraid, because I know that as I grow older, the closer I am and everyone I know and love and care for is to death. It's a fact of life. And then I remember: it's not a matter of growing older. <strong>Everyone</strong> is close to death, myself included. No one can escape it, and the only question we can ask (and will probably never get the answer until we are right there at that moment) is <em>
  <strong>When?</strong>
</em><br /><br /><em>A Grief Observed</em> doesn't have an answer to any of what I said, unfortunately. I knew C.S. Lewis was a great writer, but this book is not like any book I've read before. I can't empathize because like I said, I haven't lost anyone very close to me to death just yet (and I'm very grateful for that of course), so I read this as if I was a spectator. It almost feels like I was intruding into something very private, as if I wasn't supposed to be reading them. These are the thoughts of a man who has lost the love of his life to something he can't fight. These are the ramblings of a man who has a solid foundation for his faith yet he couldn't find foothold now that he experienced this big blow. This is a man who is grieving, period.<br /><br />I don't think anyone can ever explain how it is to grieve. I believe, like falling in love (yes, I have to connect it to that), everyone has their own process of grieving. Crying, writing, hiding yourself -- what works for you. Like death, no one is exempt from grief, but I think we do have a choice on what to believe while we grieve. Do we believe that the other person is already in a better place? Do we believe that he/she is at peace? Do we believe that God has them? Do we believe that death actually exists? What would you believe in?<br /><br />I've written so much, but I think this is one of the hardest reviews I've ever written. There's so much in <em>A Grief Observed</em> that can be said, that can be quoted, that can be criticized, even, but not so much words to write on what it is really about. It's unlike any other non-fiction book I read, and maybe this is because it's raw, and it really comes from the author's heart. This is probably the first book that I couldn't really relate to, yet I also could at the same time. Perhaps C.S. Lewis wasn't just grieving about his wife, but maybe he is also grieving about his faith, and his primitive notions of how he sees God and His love? I'm just speculating. But if that is right, then I also grieve with him for the same reasons.<br /><br /><a title="Kuya Doni" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/2156200-k-d" rel="nofollow noopener">Kuya Doni</a> is right: this book is <strong>heartwrenching.</strong> I'm glad I read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1314711170445718259" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1843454130" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1843454130" />
    <a title="Sherry Elmer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20805054-sherry-elmer"><img alt="Sherry Elmer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369803487p2/20805054.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1843454130?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sherry Elmer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sherry Elmer" href="/user/show/20805054-sherry-elmer">Sherry Elmer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060652388" href="/book/show/49221.A_Grief_Observed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20805054-sherry-elmer?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20805054-sherry-elmer?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20805054-sherry-elmer?shelf=c-s-lewis">c-s-lewis</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20805054-sherry-elmer?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1843454130" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17189951616192141880">I appreciate that this book is titled “A” Grief Observed, and not “Grief Observed;” that Lewis attempts to describe the specific grief he suffers after the death of his wife, and that he is not attempting to describe all grief. I believe it is due to detailing this particular grief that Lewis has managed to write a universally useful observation that allows the reader to relate to the thoughts and anguish found in these pages. The very fact that he isn't attempting to describe my grief, but only</span>
  <span id="freeText17189951616192141880" style="display:none">I appreciate that this book is titled “A” Grief Observed, and not “Grief Observed;” that Lewis attempts to describe the specific grief he suffers after the death of his wife, and that he is not attempting to describe all grief. I believe it is due to detailing this particular grief that Lewis has managed to write a universally useful observation that allows the reader to relate to the thoughts and anguish found in these pages. The very fact that he isn't attempting to describe my grief, but only his, allows me to benefit from his description of this universal malady. <br /><br />And describe it he does, in a deep and profound way. This small volume is packed with quote-worthy ideas. One of these is:<br /><br />“God is not trying an experiment on my faith or love in order to find out their quality. He knew it already. It was I who didn't. In this trial He makes us occupy the dock, the witness box, and the bench all at once. He always knew that my temple was a house of cards. His only way of making me realize the fact was to knock it down.”<br /><br />And this heart-wrenching quote: “Getting over it so soon? But the words are ambiguous. To say the patient is getting over it after an operation for appendicitis is one thing; after he's had his leg off it is quite another. After that operation either the wounded stump heals or the man dies. If it heals, the fierce, continuous pain will stop. Presently he'll get back his strength and be able to stump about on his wooden leg. He has 'got over it.' But he will probably have recurrent pains in the stump all his life, and perhaps pretty bad ones; and he will always be a one-legged man. There will be hardly any moment when he forgets it. Bathing, dressing, sitting down and getting up again, even lying in bed, will all be different. His whole way of life will be changed. All sorts of pleasures and activities that he once took for granted will have to be simply written off. Duties too. At present I am learning to get about on crutches. Perhaps I shall presently be given a wooden leg. But I shall never be a biped again.”<br /><br />I can't say what it would be like to read this during a period of fresh bereavement, because that is not where I am at the moment. I am in a time of grieving, but it is not grief for a loved one's death. Whether this book would be less useful (or merely more painful) at a time like that, I don't know. But I do know that as someone who has known grief, and who knows life well enough to know that I will know it again in a new and fresh way (unless I should first die suddenly), this book is helpful in taking a subject that will be experienced by almost every living person and making it accessible, and showing the reader she is not alone.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17189951616192141880" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/707853065?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/1895570-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553274868" href="/book/show/541737.A_Grief_Observed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=essays">essays</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer707853065" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10343812592412389536">It is difficult for a practicing Christian to write a book about losing a beloved wife without sounding a bit too pat to someone whose beliefs are different. And yet, I do think Lewis was honest with himself: When his wife Joy died of cancer, his recovery involved a kind of hide and seek with God. <br/><br/>Throughout this short book, Lewis maintains his high standards of writing and comes up with such painfully honest observations as the following:<blockquote>Tonight all the hells of young grief have opened again; t</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText10343812592412389536" style="display:none">It is difficult for a practicing Christian to write a book about losing a beloved wife without sounding a bit too pat to someone whose beliefs are different. And yet, I do think Lewis was honest with himself: When his wife Joy died of cancer, his recovery involved a kind of hide and seek with God. <br /><br />Throughout this short book, Lewis maintains his high standards of writing and comes up with such painfully honest observations as the following:<blockquote>Tonight all the hells of young grief have opened again; the mad words, the bitter resentment, the fluttering in the stomach, the nightmare unreality, the wallowed-in tears. For in grief nothing "stays put." One keeps on emerging from a phase, but it always recurs. Round and round. Everything repeats. Am I going in circles, or dare I hope I am on a spiral?</blockquote>It is fortunate that I have never lost the woman I love. My losses are my forebears: My father, mother, and great-grandmother. To cope with their loss, which was most harsh for me, I wound up internalizing them, seeing them as part of the person I have become, and turning inside toward myself to ask for their advice.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10343812592412389536" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5120088325725708910">I read this once before, right after college and appreciated the keen observations of God and man. I read it again just now and appreciate the deep humanness of the pain and agony. I believe this to be Lewis's best book in that it differs so dramatically from his others. It is so rich and honest and painful and hopeful. </span>

<div id="review_1487502320" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1487502320" />
    <a title="Demetrius Rogers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5155177-demetrius-rogers"><img alt="Demetrius Rogers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413944499p2/5155177.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1487502320?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Demetrius Rogers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Demetrius Rogers" href="/user/show/5155177-demetrius-rogers">Demetrius Rogers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060652388" href="/book/show/49221.A_Grief_Observed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5155177-demetrius-rogers?shelf=soul-care">soul-care</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5155177-demetrius-rogers?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5155177-demetrius-rogers?shelf=spirituality">spirituality</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1487502320" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer352663131857710274">This was one of the greatest books I've read this year. Man! This was outstanding. Wow. I can't say enough about the wisdom and insight embedded in these pages. I love short, pithy, and thoughtful works. Concentrated thought can go so far. Add a little water and it expands into a meal. There might be more here than in a book of a 1,000 pages. <br/><br/>My favorite writing of Lewis' is his novel Till We Have Faces. TWHF is about the grief that follows loss, and in this story Lewis nails it in stunning fash</span>
  <span id="freeText352663131857710274" style="display:none">This was one of the greatest books I've read this year. Man! This was outstanding. Wow. I can't say enough about the wisdom and insight embedded in these pages. I love short, pithy, and thoughtful works. Concentrated thought can go so far. Add a little water and it expands into a meal. There might be more here than in a book of a 1,000 pages. <br /><br />My favorite writing of Lewis' is his novel Till We Have Faces. TWHF is about the grief that follows loss, and in this story Lewis nails it in stunning fashion! TWHF was published in 1956, and later that year he suffered his own loss - the death of his wife. So I find it incredibly interesting that Lewis, after writing TWHF, should have to live it out in his own life. <br /><br />Lament is a curious honor the Lord bequeaths upon us. That God should countenance such raw honesty is amazing. But, as we see in Scripture, he does! He even endorses it. And lament (the honesty) helps us to heal and wise up. Lewis goes from (it would seem) near blasphemy (at least, insult) to a clear-minded joy and praise of life and its Maker. This, for sure, deserves multiple readings. <br /><br />I love books that draw me close to God. And this one did.</span>
  <a data-text-id="352663131857710274" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2712562763" />
    <a title="Anna Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4237800-anna-maria"><img alt="Anna Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483520325p2/4237800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2712562763?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna Maria" href="/user/show/4237800-anna-maria">Anna Maria</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060652388" href="/book/show/49221.A_Grief_Observed">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2712562763" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer946752994472171829">This book on loss totally captured me. I lost my father many years ago and this book reflects what I proved losing him to cancer. "No one ever told me that grief felt so like fear" a sentence that Lewis writes in this book absolutely describes what I felt, I lost my dad when I still needed him and even today that I am a mother I miss him terribly.<br/>Some of the parts are so heartbreaking and sad that I was totally captured right from the beginning till the end. This book surely has a great message.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_62034767" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62034767" />
    <a title="Nick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2393718-nick"><img alt="Nick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1447934801p2/2393718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62034767?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick" href="/user/show/2393718-nick">Nick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060652388" href="/book/show/49221.A_Grief_Observed">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2393718-nick?shelf=theology">theology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2393718-nick?shelf=christian-living">christian-living</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62034767" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13855032574871532191">I have had some grief in my life but nothing earth-shattering. I've had grandparents, uncles and aunts, friends, and loads of pets die; however, I have never felt the extreme loss that Lewis touches on so briefly and yet so poignantly here. I had no real reason to pick it up when I did, other than I had never read it and I wanted to read something by Lewis. Now, after reading it, I have two thoughts on this book: <br/><br/>1.) Part of me cannot fully appreciate the weight of this book until I go through m</span>
  <span id="freeText13855032574871532191" style="display:none">I have had some grief in my life but nothing earth-shattering. I've had grandparents, uncles and aunts, friends, and loads of pets die; however, I have never felt the extreme loss that Lewis touches on so briefly and yet so poignantly here. I had no real reason to pick it up when I did, other than I had never read it and I wanted to read something by Lewis. Now, after reading it, I have two thoughts on this book: <br /><br />1.) Part of me cannot fully appreciate the weight of this book until I go through my own intense grieving process. As of right now, I am a casual observer--a sympath rather than an empath. With time this relationship will change. Hopefully, it will grow even higher in my estimation.<br /><br />2.) Lewis gives more than a window into grief. He gives personal, theological, apologetic insights with disarming skill because he carries the reader along with him as he wrestles with God. <br /><br />It wasn't a fun read, but I loved it none-the-less. Definitely a reread or two in the future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13855032574871532191" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FS/70pOFqsg2gJ5lHxQOl11i+lf5HF/mhiGLykPyq2/nkx76p+chrqivk6fYgk7g0EUZ5veFbZIOJfkYZrt+rw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5jB8v1zCTMxyqH6aOHhcRx64rYje933Cnt1QrJ8OJf4UjJmXaKDHquyHc1j/7hwwk59OOdBuT7YW2SJ+ukfwPg==')})
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_174834'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/174834.The_Cost_of_Discipleship?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Cost of Discipleship<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/29333.Dietrich_Bonhoeffer?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Dietrich Bonhoeffer<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.28 avg rating &mdash; 35,334 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1937\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer17311303290598252564\">One of the most important theologians of the twentieth century illuminates the relationship between ourselves and the teachings of Jesus in this classic text on ethics, humanism, and civic duty.What can the call to discipleship, the adherence to the <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17311303290598252564\" style=\"display:none\">One of the most important theologians of the twentieth century illuminates the relationship between ourselves and the teachings of Jesus in this classic text on ethics, humanism, and civic duty.What can the call to discipleship, the adherence to the word of Jesus, mean today to the businessman, the soldier, the laborer, or the aristocrat? What did Jesus mean to say to us? What is his will for us today? Drawing on the Sermon on the Mount, Dietrich Bonhoeffer answers these timeless questions by providing a seminal reading of the dichotomy between &quot;cheap grace&quot; and &quot;costly grace.&quot; &quot;Cheap grace,&quot; Bonhoeffer wrote, &quot;is the grace we bestow on ourselves...grace without discipleship....Costly grace is the gospel which must be sought again and again, the girl which must be asked for, the door at which a man must know....It is costly because it costs a man his life, and it is grace because it gives a man the only true life.&quot;The Cost of Discipleship is a Gordon alone survived. And forgave. He said of the bombers, \' I have lost my daughter, but I bear no g<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2212297603543648330\" style=\"display:none\">Original Cover Edition for ISBN 0310245656\n\nIn 1987, an IRA bomb buried Gordon Wilson and his twenty-year-old daughter beneath five feet of rubble. Gordon alone survived. And forgave. He said of the bombers, \' I have lost my daughter, but I bear no grudge . . . I shall pray, tonight and every night, that God will forgive them.\' His words caught the media\'s ears  and out of one man\'s grief, the world got a glimpse of grace. Grace is the church\'s great distinctive. It\'s the one thing the world cannot duplicate, and the one thing it craves above all else  for only grace can bring hope and transformation to a jaded world. In What\'s So Amazing About Grace? award-winning author Philip Yancey explores grace at street level. If grace is God\'s love for the undeserving, he asks, then what does it look like in action? And if Christians are its sole dispensers, then how are we doing at lavishing grace on a world that knows far more of cruelty and unforgiveness than it does of mercy? Yancey sets grace in the midst of life\'s stark images, tests its mettle against horrific \'ungrace.\' Can grace survive in the midst of such atrocities as the Nazi holocaust? Can it triumph over the brutality of the Ku Klux Klan? Should any grace at all be shown to the likes of Jeffrey Dahmer, who killed and cannibalized seventeen young men? Grace does not excuse sin, says Yancey, but it treasures the sinner. True grace is shocking, scandalous. It shakes our conventions with its insistence on getting close to sinners and touching them with mercy and hope. It forgives the unfaithful spouse, the racist, the child abuser. It loves today\'s AIDS-ridden addict as much as the tax collector of Jesus\' day. In his most personal and provocative book ever, Yancey offers compelling, true portraits of grace\'s life-changing power. He searches for its presence in his own life and in the church. He asks, How can Christians contend graciously with moral issues that threaten all they hold dear? And he challenges us to become living answers to a world that desperately wants to know, What\'s So Amazing About Grace?<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"2212297603543648330\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1069006.C_S_Lewis">About C.S. Lewis</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1069006.C_S_Lewis"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1564671804p3/1069006.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14468447605084645735"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br/><br/><br/>Clive Staples Lewis was one of the intellectual giants of the twentieth century and arguably one of the most influential writers of his day. He was a Fellow and Tutor in English Literature at Oxford University until 1954. He was unanimously elected to the Chair of Medieval and Renaissance Literature at Cambridge</span>
  <span id="freeText14468447605084645735" style="display:none"><i>Librarian Note: There is more than one author in the Goodreads database with this name.</i><br /><br /><br />Clive Staples Lewis was one of the intellectual giants of the twentieth century and arguably one of the most influential writers of his day. He was a Fellow and Tutor in English Literature at Oxford University until 1954. He was unanimously elected to the Chair of Medieval and Renaissance Literature at Cambridge University, a position he held until his retirement. He wrote more than thirty books, allowing him to reach a vast audience, and his works continue to attract thousands of new readers every year. His most distinguished and popular accomplishments include <i>Mere Christianity</i>, <i>Out of the Silent Planet</i>, <i>The Great Divorce</i>, <i>The Screwtape Letters</i>, and the universally acknowledged classics <i>The Chronicles of Narnia</i>. To date, the Narnia books have sold over 100 million copies and been transformed into three major motion pictures.<br /><br />Lewis was married to poet <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/467360.Joy_Davidman" title="Joy Davidman" rel="nofollow noopener">Joy Davidman</a>.<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1988716._W_H__Lewis" title=" W.H. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener"> W.H. Lewis</a> was his elder brother]</span>
  <a data-text-id="14468447605084645735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
